<td colspan="2" style="text-align: left">
<p><i>Aquila heliaca heliaca</i>
</p>
</td></tr>

</tbody></table>
<p>The <b>eastern imperial eagle</b> (<i>Aquila heliaca</i>) is a large <a href="/wiki/Bird_of_prey" title="Bird of prey">bird of prey</a> that breeds in <a href="/wiki/Southeastern_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Southeastern Europe">southeastern Europe</a> and extensively through West and <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>. Most populations are migratory and winter in northeastern <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South</a> and <a href="/wiki/East_Asia" title="East Asia">East Asia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Like all <a href="/wiki/Eagle" title="Eagle">eagles</a>, the eastern imperial eagle is a member of the family <a href="/wiki/Accipitridae" title="Accipitridae">Accipitridae</a>. Furthermore, its well feathered legs mark it as a member of the subfamily <a href="/wiki/Aquilinae" title="Aquilinae">Aquilinae</a>. It is a large dark colored eagle, with a resemblance to other members of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Aquila_(genus)" title="Aquila (genus)">Aquila</a></i> but it is usually the darkest species in its range.<sup id="cite_ref-Forsman_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forsman-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> This is an opportunistic predator that mostly selects smallish <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a> as prey but also a fairly large proportion of <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">reptile</a> and other prey types, including <a href="/wiki/Carrion" title="Carrion">carrion</a>. Compared to other <i>Aquila</i> eagles, it has a strong preference for the interface of tall woods with plains and other open, relatively flat habitats. Normally, nests are located in large, mature trees and the parents raise around one or two fledglings.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HBW_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> The global population is small and declining due to persecution, loss of habitat and prey. It has therefore been <a href="/wiki/IUCN_Red_List" title="IUCN Red List">IUCN Red Listed</a> as <a href="/wiki/Vulnerable_species" title="Vulnerable species">Vulnerable</a> since 1994.<sup id="cite_ref-iucn_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iucn-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy">Taxonomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_imperial_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Taxonomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The eastern imperial eagle is a member of the <a href="/wiki/Aquilinae" title="Aquilinae">Aquilinae</a> or booted eagles, a rather <a href="/wiki/Monophyly" title="Monophyly">monophyletic</a> subfamily of the accipitrid family. At least 38 species are currently housed in the subfamily, all with signature well-feathered tarsi. This species is a member of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Aquila_(genus)" title="Aquila (genus)">Aquila</a></i>, which are mostly large, fairly dark colored eagles distributed largely through the more open habitats of <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">Eurasia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a> (with one in <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a> and a couple in <a href="/wiki/Australasia" title="Australasia">Australasia</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Lerner_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lerner-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Studies on DNA have indicated that the imperial eagle is part of a subgroup with other moderately sized <i>Aquila</i> such as the <a href="/wiki/Steppe_eagle" title="Steppe eagle">steppe eagle</a> (<i>Aquila nipalensis</i>) and the <a href="/wiki/Tawny_eagle" title="Tawny eagle">tawny eagle</a> (<i>Aquila rapax</i>). Despite the  outward resemblance to the imperial eagle, the 4 species in the <a href="/wiki/Golden_eagle" title="Golden eagle">golden eagle</a> subgroup appear to rather more closely related to the dissimilarly smallish and pale-bellied <a href="/wiki/Species_complex" title="Species complex">sister species</a>, <a href="/wiki/African_hawk_eagle" class="mw-redirect" title="African hawk eagle">African hawk eagle</a> (<i>Aquila spilogaster</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Bonelli%27s_eagle" title="Bonelli&#39;s eagle">Bonelli's eagle</a> (<i>Aquila fasicata</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Spanish_imperial_eagle" title="Spanish imperial eagle">Spanish imperial eagle</a>, which is found in <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Portugal" title="Portugal">Portugal</a>, was formerly <a href="/wiki/Lumpers_and_splitters#Lumping_and_splitting_in_biology" title="Lumpers and splitters">lumped</a> with this species, the name imperial eagle having been previously used in both circumstances. However, the two are now regarded as separate species<sup id="cite_ref-Sangster2002_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sangster2002-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> due to significant differences in <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">morphology</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Cramp_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cramp-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ecology" title="Ecology">ecology</a><sup id="cite_ref-HBW_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> and molecular characteristics.<sup id="cite_ref-Padilla1999_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Padilla1999-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Seibold1996_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Seibold1996-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> It is likely that the eastern imperial eagle is the <a href="/wiki/Paraspecies" title="Paraspecies">paraspecies</a> for the Spanish imperial eagle and that the imperial eagle complex reached the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_peninsula" class="mw-redirect" title="Iberian peninsula">Iberian peninsula</a> sometimes between the late <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene_era" class="mw-redirect" title="Pleistocene era">Pleistocene era</a> and early <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a>. The Spanish imperial eagle may be considered an <a href="/wiki/Glacial_relict" title="Glacial relict">ice age relict</a> due to its isolation.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_imperial_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Description">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kaiseradler_Aquila_heliaca_2_amk.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Kaiseradler_Aquila_heliaca_2_amk.jpg/220px-Kaiseradler_Aquila_heliaca_2_amk.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="329" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Kaiseradler_Aquila_heliaca_2_amk.jpg/330px-Kaiseradler_Aquila_heliaca_2_amk.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Kaiseradler_Aquila_heliaca_2_amk.jpg/440px-Kaiseradler_Aquila_heliaca_2_amk.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1473" data-file-height="2200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kaiseradler_Aquila_heliaca_2_amk.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Closeup of an adult imperial eagle.</div></div></div>
<p>The eastern imperial eagle is quite a large <a href="/wiki/Eagle" title="Eagle">eagle</a> but usually falls near the middle of sizes in the large-bodied genus <i><a href="/wiki/Aquila_(genus)" title="Aquila (genus)">Aquila</a></i>. Adult total length can range from 68 to 90&#160;cm (27 to 35&#160;in) with a typical wingspan of 1.76 to 2.16&#160;m (5&#160;ft 9&#160;in to 7&#160;ft 1&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ali_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ali-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> The average wingspan of a small sample showed males to average 1.95&#160;m (6&#160;ft 5&#160;in) while a small sample of females averaged 2.07&#160;m (6&#160;ft 9&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Although otherwise outwardly similar, the species displays <a href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism" title="Sexual dimorphism">reverse sexual dimorphism</a> as do most <a href="/wiki/Birds_of_prey" class="mw-redirect" title="Birds of prey">birds of prey</a>, in which males are usually smaller than the females. For the eastern imperial eagle, females are up to 10% larger linearly and 40% heavier in body mass in some cases.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dimorphism_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dimorphism-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> In terms of body mass, one survey found five males to weigh from 2.45 to 2.72&#160;kg (5.4 to 6.0&#160;lb) and  five females to weigh from 3.26 to 4.54&#160;kg (7.2 to 10.0&#160;lb). The average weight of this sample of imperial eagles was reportedly 2.62&#160;kg (5.8&#160;lb) in the males and 3.9&#160;kg (8.6&#160;lb) in the females.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CRC_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> A sample of unknown size showed males to weigh an average 2.88&#160;kg (6.3&#160;lb) while females reportedly weighed an average of 3.38&#160;kg (7.5&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Kirmse_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kirmse-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> A further two mature females weighed an average of 3.56&#160;kg (7.8&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Among standard measurements, males may range in <a href="/wiki/Wing_chord_(biology)" title="Wing chord (biology)">wing chord</a> length from 540 to 622&#160;mm (21.3 to 24.5&#160;in), in <a href="/wiki/Tail" title="Tail">tail</a> length from 260 to 308&#160;mm (10.2 to 12.1&#160;in) and in <a href="/wiki/Tibiotarsus" title="Tibiotarsus">tarsus</a> length from 91 to 98&#160;mm (3.6 to 3.9&#160;in). Meanwhile, females may range in wing chord length from 565 to 665&#160;mm (22.2 to 26.2&#160;in), in tail length from 270 to 330&#160;mm (11 to 13&#160;in) and in tarsus length from 97 to 107&#160;mm (3.8 to 4.2&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cramp_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cramp-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Imperial_Eagle_(Aquila_heliaca)_(38741311735).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Imperial_Eagle_%28Aquila_heliaca%29_%2838741311735%29.jpg/220px-Imperial_Eagle_%28Aquila_heliaca%29_%2838741311735%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Imperial_Eagle_%28Aquila_heliaca%29_%2838741311735%29.jpg/330px-Imperial_Eagle_%28Aquila_heliaca%29_%2838741311735%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Imperial_Eagle_%28Aquila_heliaca%29_%2838741311735%29.jpg/440px-Imperial_Eagle_%28Aquila_heliaca%29_%2838741311735%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="1500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Imperial_Eagle_(Aquila_heliaca)_(38741311735).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An eastern imperial eagle, probably an older subadult, in flight showing their characteristic flat, relatively narrow wings.</div></div></div>
<p>In general, compared to other species in their taxonomic group, the eastern imperial eagle has a relatively long and thick neck, a big head and bill (with a gape line level with middle of eye), a longish square tipped tail, somewhat long and well-feathered legs and strong feet. The species tends to perch in a fairly upright position often on rather exposed tree branch or low mound, rock, haystack or similar convenient site. For an <i>Aquila</i> eagle, it is seemingly relatively less shy and bolder in the presence of humans.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The adult plumage is largely a <a href="/wiki/Tar" title="Tar">tar</a>-like blackish-brown but for a well-demarcated and highly contrasting creamy to golden buff colour about the crown, hindneck and neck sides. Furthermore, adults have bold white spots on their shoulder braces, which are usually fairly conspicuous on perched birds. The adult’s tail is narrowly dark barred over a greyish ground colour and has a broad black subterminal band, while a white tail tip sometimes manifest in adults that are freshly molted. The undertail coverts are sometimes indistinctly paler, rust to creamy, combined with grey tail base to give the appearance of a paler rear end.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Forsman_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forsman-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Clark_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clark-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Forsman2_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forsman2-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> At rest, the wing tips tend to reach the tail tip.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The juvenile eastern imperial eagle is mostly pale tawny-buff to sandy yellow with fairly heavy dark brown streaks from the throat down to the breast, mantle, scapulars and forewing coverts. The juvenile’s scapulars and forewing coverts also have sometimes noticeable white tipped feathers while the median coverts are perceptibly browner and greater coverts blackish both with broad creamy-yellow tips forming clear wing bars. The flight feathers and tail on juveniles are often blackish and tipped whitish, however the white parts on the lower back to tail coverts are only lightly streaked in the centre and often not visible when perched. Below the streaked breast, the remainder of the juvenile’s underparts are plain pale buff. By the 2nd year, the brown streaking on the underside fades to a plainer sandy hue and the pale bars also start to fade on wings. Especially later into the 2nd year into the 3rd year, some young eastern imperial-eagles show an erratic blotching of blackish-brown feathers below. By about the 4th winter, as the birds enter their subadult plumage, the forefront of the eagle is often a rather patchy mix of sandy and darker adult-like feathers. During slow annual molts, the dark feathers expand initially from the throat and upper breast outward. Late into the subadult stage, the birds also start to develop a pale crown and nape but usually the rear body still more juvenile-like, such as the pale rump-band and crissum despite the otherwise darkening tail and wing feathers. The full adult plumage is attained at 5–6 years of age but some subadults are already breeding before this.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Forsman_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forsman-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Clark_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clark-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Forsman2_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forsman2-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bijleveld_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bijleveld-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aquila_heliaca,_Hong_Kong_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Aquila_heliaca%2C_Hong_Kong_1.jpg/220px-Aquila_heliaca%2C_Hong_Kong_1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Aquila_heliaca%2C_Hong_Kong_1.jpg/330px-Aquila_heliaca%2C_Hong_Kong_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Aquila_heliaca%2C_Hong_Kong_1.jpg/440px-Aquila_heliaca%2C_Hong_Kong_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="768" data-file-height="1024" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aquila_heliaca,_Hong_Kong_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An adult imperial eagle in <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In flight, the eastern imperial eagle is a large raptor that has for an <i>Aquila</i> a very projecting neck and "huge" head and bill.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Clark_22-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clark-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> The long wings may appear fairly broad when compared to other, smaller raptors, but are relatively narrow with even parallel edges when compared to other <i>Aquila</i> eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-Forsman_3-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forsman-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Forsman2_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forsman2-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Their flight style is relatively heavy but steady with deep powerful beats but they are not uncommonly clumsy at first takeoff.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> They tend to soar with forward pressed but rather flat wings, the outer wing feathers may sometimes curve up but as a rule they do not fly with a <a href="/wiki/Dihedral_(aeronautics)" title="Dihedral (aeronautics)">V</a> as do some other <i>Aquila</i>. The species may also hold their wings flat while engaging in a glide but as it accelerates they may arch wings back. In flight, the adult from above shows dark brown with small white braces, greyish tail with fine dark bars and a very broad, blackish subterminal band. The adult is essentially all dark colored when seen from below relieved only by some very dusky grey flight feathers on the primaries (against the black wing tips), a grey crissum and thinly barred grey tail base. However, these features may be only obvious in good light and at reasonable distances.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Forsman2_23-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forsman2-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Porter_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Porter-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Forsman3_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forsman3-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> In flight, the juvenile is largely pale buff with brown streaking. The lower back, rump, tail coverts and leg feathers are all whitish cream in colour which contrasts noticeably with their white-tipped blackish greater coverts, primary coverts and quills. On their mantle, juveniles manifest two white wing bars above and a narrow whitish lower trailing edge. Meanwhile, the juvenile’s inner primaries are much paler creamy-buff hue than their other flight feathers. The dusky brown wing linings of the juvenile plumage, when compared to the darker ones of adults, show more extensive coarse patterning. Some birds by 2nd to 3rd year are so worn in their flight feathers as to appear almost unstreaked sandy while, from the 3rd year on, darker feathers start to appear below with various untidy variations.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Porter_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Porter-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Forsman2_23-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forsman2-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Confusion_of_species">Confusion of species</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_imperial_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Confusion of species">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Zoo19feb_(65)a.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Zoo19feb_%2865%29a.JPG/220px-Zoo19feb_%2865%29a.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="303" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Zoo19feb_%2865%29a.JPG/330px-Zoo19feb_%2865%29a.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Zoo19feb_%2865%29a.JPG/440px-Zoo19feb_%2865%29a.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1362" data-file-height="1875" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Zoo19feb_(65)a.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A captive adult eastern imperial eagle sharing an aviary with a smaller cousin, the <a href="/wiki/Lesser_spotted_eagle" title="Lesser spotted eagle">lesser spotted eagle</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The eastern imperial eagle is typically smaller, being more slender and less bulky and powerful in appearance despite its proportionately larger head and longer neck, than the <a href="/wiki/Golden_eagle" title="Golden eagle">golden eagle</a> (<i>Aquila chrysaetos</i>). In some parts of the range, the female imperial eagle probably averages similar size and body mass to the smaller male golden eagle. However, apparently the species can be reliably distinguished by the size of its <a href="/wiki/Claw" title="Claw">hallux claw</a> (the enlarged rear talon that accipitrids used as a killing apparatus) which is consistently larger in the golden species. Two female eastern imperial eagles measured 38 and 43&#160;mm (1.5 and 1.7&#160;in) in hallux claw length while a male was about 35.5&#160;mm (1.40&#160;in), whereas the smallest talon measured for an immature male American golden eagle was 44.9&#160;mm (1.77&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Nonetheless, besides its wider ranging golden cousin, this species is typically the largest, with the largest extremities (i.e. tarsal, tail and bill length) and most powerful <a href="/wiki/Aquilinae" title="Aquilinae">booted eagle</a> species in the majority of its range (apart from its rare African winter range) when compared to similar eagles such as others in the genera <i><a href="/wiki/Aquila_(genus)" title="Aquila (genus)">Aquila</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Clanga_(genus)" title="Clanga (genus)">Clanga</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Forsman_3-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forsman-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Clark_22-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clark-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Usually, the plumage of the adult eastern imperial eagle is very distinctive. It is considerably darker than other adult <i>Aquila</i> eagles in central Eurasia. Furthermore, all other Eurasian eagles in their range lack the white spots on the wing mantle and greyish under-tail.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Forsman_3-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forsman-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Clark_22-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clark-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Given reasonable views, the juvenile imperial eagle is no less distinctive, with its unique tawny-buff covered in brownish streaking, a colour combination not seen in other species.<sup id="cite_ref-Clark_22-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clark-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> From a distance, the juvenile may give the impression of a dark mantle and chest band with very pale rear body and a blackish tail and <a href="/wiki/Flight_feather" title="Flight feather">remiges</a> against strikingly pale primary wedges.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Adults do however strongly resemble their considerably extralimital cousins, the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_imperial_eagle" title="Spanish imperial eagle">Spanish imperial eagle</a>, but the eastern species has more restricted white on the shoulder and has a slightly more brownish hue in the dark underside feathers, while juvenile Spanish imperials are richly tawny in colour rather than pale buffy and lack brownish streaking on the body. The Spanish species is similar in size and proportions to the eastern imperial but is marginally heavier on average and has an even more protruding head and neck.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HBW_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The subadult eastern imperial eagle may be confused with older immature <a href="/wiki/Greater_spotted_eagle" title="Greater spotted eagle">greater spotted eagles</a> (<i>Clanga clanga</i>) but the latter is less contrastingly patterned, without a paler shawl and has greater covert band below (despite the rare hint of one on some imperials) and densely bared flight feathers. Adult greater spotted eagles have similar underwing contrast as juvenile eastern imperial eagles but, beyond plumage characteristics, are distinct for their much smaller head, slightly smaller overall size and relatively broader and shorter wings.<sup id="cite_ref-Forsman_3-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forsman-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Indian_spotted_eagle" title="Indian spotted eagle">Indian spotted eagles</a> (<i>Clanga hastata</i>) tend to show paler lesser under-wing coverts like juvenile eastern imperial eagles but are considerably smaller and differ in all other proportions and plumage features.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Although at times described as “very similar”, the eastern imperial eagles are fairly easily told from the <a href="/wiki/Golden_eagle" title="Golden eagle">golden eagle</a> by being much darker with less broad wings that are held much flatter. The golden eagle, unlike imperial eagles, tends to fly with its wings pointed upwards and have a gradual tapering wing shape (somewhat narrower at the base, broader at the primaries). In golden eagles, the tail generally appears narrower and squarer in shape. At closer range, the pale area on the back of the head and neck in eastern imperial eagle may be suggestive to some of the golden eagle’s golden nape but is always much paler and chalkier in colour, as well as more strongly contrasted by the otherwise dark feathers and is more extensive.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Clark_22-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clark-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Against the somewhat similarly sized but more compact juvenile <a href="/wiki/Steppe_eagle" title="Steppe eagle">steppe eagle</a> (<i>Aquila nipalensis</i>), the juvenile eastern imperial eagle does not show a white band on underwing like the steppe. More obviously, the steppe eagle lacks the paler overall colours and contrasting brownish streaking of juvenile imperials. Feather wear can make the wing shape of the two resemble the other but the larger head and less compact frame of the imperial species render its flight profile distinctive.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The only darker large booted eagle encountered by the eastern imperial eagle, in its African winter quarters, is the jet-black <a href="/wiki/Verreaux%27s_eagle" title="Verreaux&#39;s eagle">Verreaux's eagle</a> (<i>Aquila verreauxii</i>) which is more suggestive in size and proportions of the golden eagle. An unlikely source of confusion, the Verreaux's differs in almost all plumage characteristics and has far more tapered wings that pinch in at the base and, like the golden eagle, tends to fly in a fairly strong <a href="/wiki/Dihedral_(aeronautics)" title="Dihedral (aeronautics)">dihedral</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a>, its proportions and size may be suggestive of the perhaps even rarer <a href="/wiki/Pallas%27s_fish_eagle" title="Pallas&#39;s fish eagle">Pallas's fish eagle</a> (<i>Haliaeetus leucoryphus</i>) and the two can potentially be mistaken in strongly backlit conditions which obscure their obviously distinct plumages.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The large headed and flat winged shape of the imperial eagle may too suggest in silhouette the <a href="/wiki/White-tailed_eagle" title="White-tailed eagle">white-tailed eagle</a> (<i>Haliaeetus albicilla</i>) but that species is usually rather larger bodied and even larger headed with much broader wings and a proportionately shorter tail.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Forsman3_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forsman3-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cinereous_vulture" title="Cinereous vulture">Cinereous vulture</a> (<i>Aegypius monachus</i>), being similarly dusky overall below, have been suggested as confusion species, but are much larger than imperial eagles with differing proportions (far broader wings, proportionately smaller head and shorter tail) and lack any of the contrasting pale parts of the imperial eagle’s plumage.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vocalizations">Vocalizations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_imperial_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Vocalizations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kaiseradler_Aquila_heliaca_4_amk.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Kaiseradler_Aquila_heliaca_4_amk.jpg/220px-Kaiseradler_Aquila_heliaca_4_amk.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="158" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Kaiseradler_Aquila_heliaca_4_amk.jpg/330px-Kaiseradler_Aquila_heliaca_4_amk.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Kaiseradler_Aquila_heliaca_4_amk.jpg/440px-Kaiseradler_Aquila_heliaca_4_amk.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3472" data-file-height="2492" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kaiseradler_Aquila_heliaca_4_amk.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A probable older subadult eastern imperial eagle in a captive <a href="/wiki/Falconry" title="Falconry">falconry</a> centre.</div></div></div>
<p>The eastern imperial eagle is quite vocal on their breeding ground but tends to be silent or nearly so elsewhere. The main call of the species is a deep, harsh bark <i>owk owk</i>, <i>gok gok</i> or <i>kraw-kraw</i>. The call tends to be repeated rapidly up to 8-10 times. Their call is perceptibly deeper and harsher than that of the <a href="/wiki/Golden_eagle" title="Golden eagle">golden eagle</a>, somewhat incongruously also being more resonant and commanding. Sometimes when extending to a prolonged version, its call is sometimes considered reminiscent of the croak of a large <a href="/wiki/Frog" title="Frog">frog</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gould_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gould-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> The eastern imperial eagle most frequently calls during their aerial displays. In extreme cases, the call may be repeated up to 13 times during an aerial display. Furthermore, they may repeat the call from a perch. Females may also call when a male arrives with prey. Alarm calls recorded for the species include a soft <i>ko-gok, wk wk wk</i>, uttered in response to a distant intruder, a closer approach also may cause a harder <i>gek</i> call by the mother. A rising and falling trill has been additionally recorded in captivity is also probably an alarm call.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution_and_habitat">Distribution and habitat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_imperial_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Distribution and habitat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Breeding_range">Breeding range</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_imperial_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Breeding range">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:%D7%A2%D7%99%D7%98_%D7%A9%D7%9E%D7%A9_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/%D7%A2%D7%99%D7%98_%D7%A9%D7%9E%D7%A9_2.jpg/220px-%D7%A2%D7%99%D7%98_%D7%A9%D7%9E%D7%A9_2.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/%D7%A2%D7%99%D7%98_%D7%A9%D7%9E%D7%A9_2.jpg/330px-%D7%A2%D7%99%D7%98_%D7%A9%D7%9E%D7%A9_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/%D7%A2%D7%99%D7%98_%D7%A9%D7%9E%D7%A9_2.jpg/440px-%D7%A2%D7%99%D7%98_%D7%A9%D7%9E%D7%A9_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="813" data-file-height="543" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:%D7%A2%D7%99%D7%98_%D7%A9%D7%9E%D7%A9_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A juvenile photographed from helicopter. Eastern imperial eagles prefer the interface of woods and open areas and can tolerate agriculture and development so long as prey sources remain.</div></div></div>
<p>The eastern imperial eagle is found as far west as east-central and southeastern Europe. The European part of its breeding range includes eastern <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a>, eastern <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">Slovakia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a>, eastern <a href="/wiki/Croatia" title="Croatia">Croatia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbia</a>, northeastern <a href="/wiki/Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Bosnia and Herzegovina">Bosnia and Herzegovina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(region)" title="Macedonia (region)">Macedonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moldova" title="Moldova">Moldova</a> and northern, western and much of the eastern part of the <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-iucn_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iucn-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cramp_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cramp-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Michev_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Michev-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> The species distribution continues across central Russia, where it is found through most of the <a href="/wiki/Central_Federal_District" title="Central Federal District">Central Federal District</a>, essentially all of the <a href="/wiki/North_Caucasian_Federal_District" title="North Caucasian Federal District">North Caucasian Federal District</a>, most of the <a href="/wiki/Volga_Federal_District" title="Volga Federal District">Volga</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ural_Federal_District" title="Ural Federal District">Ural Federal Districts</a> (excluding the northern parts), and the southern part of <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a> past <a href="/wiki/Lake_Baikal" title="Lake Baikal">Lake Baikal</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Zabaykalsky_Krai" title="Zabaykalsky Krai">Transbaikalia</a> in the landlocked southwest of the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Far_East" title="Russian Far East">Russian Far East</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-iucn_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iucn-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Michev_34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Michev-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Out of Russia, their breeding extends south to mostly the northern portions of the following nations: <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkmenistan" title="Turkmenistan">Turkmenistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a>, much of <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a>, northwestern <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> (<a href="/wiki/Xinjiang" title="Xinjiang">Xinjiang</a>) and northern <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-iucn_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iucn-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HBW_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Michev_34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Michev-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bukreev_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bukreev-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Isolated populations also persist in northwestern, central and eastern <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a>, and northern <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>. As a breeding species, they are probably extirpated as from <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-iucn_1-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iucn-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Michev_34-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Michev-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Turan,_L._2005_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Turan,_L._2005-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Migration_and_wintering_range">Migration and wintering range</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_imperial_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Migration and wintering range">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aquila_heliaca,_Little_Rann_of_Kutch_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Aquila_heliaca%2C_Little_Rann_of_Kutch_1.jpg/220px-Aquila_heliaca%2C_Little_Rann_of_Kutch_1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="258" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Aquila_heliaca%2C_Little_Rann_of_Kutch_1.jpg/330px-Aquila_heliaca%2C_Little_Rann_of_Kutch_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Aquila_heliaca%2C_Little_Rann_of_Kutch_1.jpg/440px-Aquila_heliaca%2C_Little_Rann_of_Kutch_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1291" data-file-height="1516" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aquila_heliaca,_Little_Rann_of_Kutch_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A wintering imperial eagle in <a href="/wiki/Little_Rann_of_Kutch" title="Little Rann of Kutch">Little Rann of Kutch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gujarat" title="Gujarat">Gujarat</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Unlike the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_imperial_eagle" title="Spanish imperial eagle">Spanish imperial eagle</a>, the eastern imperial eagle is fairly strongly migratory in most of its range, though a variable amount of residency or very local wandering during winter in the western and southern parts of its range may lend it to be described as a partial migrant. The species has been recorded overwintering as far north as <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bukreev_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bukreev-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a>, of three post dispersal juveniles, two wandering within the country and only one migrated a long distance to <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> Migratory movements occur during fall any time from September to November and in spring anytime from February to May, shifting earlier in the fall and later in spring the farther north that the eagles breed. Differentiating the large areas use merely for passage migration or vagrancy from regular wintering grounds can be difficult.<sup id="cite_ref-iucn_1-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iucn-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HBW_4-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Domashevsk_2002_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Domashevsk_2002-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Though typically seen in very small numbers at main raptor migration sites the species may occur as a passage migrant through much of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> down to <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a>, with pockets of wintering eagles in Israel, northern <a href="/wiki/Jordan" title="Jordan">Jordan</a>, central <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kuwait" title="Kuwait">Kuwait</a>, northeastern <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a> and adjacent southwestern <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> A radio-tagging study of a few wintering eagles in <a href="/wiki/Arabia" class="mw-redirect" title="Arabia">Arabia</a> found they returned variously to <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> in four cases and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Kazkhstan&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Kazkhstan (page does not exist)">Kazkhstan</a> and <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> in a single case, with a range of ground covered in spring migration of 3,900 to 5,000&#160;km (2,400 to 3,100&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> The eastern imperial eagle winters locally and in quite small numbers in Africa’s <a href="/wiki/Nile" title="Nile">Nile valley</a>, mostly being reported in southern <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a>, central <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> and northern <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a>, irregularly down as far as southern <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a> and once even northern <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-iucn_1-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iucn-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HBW_4-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kemp_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kemp-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> Most migrants to Africa apparently originate in the western part of the breeding range such as <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Domashevsk_2002_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Domashevsk_2002-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Further east, such as the imperial eagles that breed around <a href="/wiki/Lake_Baikal" title="Lake Baikal">Lake Baikal</a>, will generally migrate to <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">south Asia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> Moderate to quite low numbers are usually noted of this species at migration sites in the <a href="/wiki/Himalayas" title="Himalayas">Himalayas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> The eastern imperial eagle winters fairly broadly in the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>, from eastern <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, eastward through southern <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a> to <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a> and down as far south in <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> as the states of <a href="/wiki/Gujarat" title="Gujarat">Gujarat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Madhya_Pradesh" title="Madhya Pradesh">Madhya Pradesh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bihar" title="Bihar">Bihar</a> and northwestern <a href="/wiki/Jharkhand" title="Jharkhand">Jharkhand</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-iucn_1-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iucn-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HBW_4-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Other semi-regular wintering areas include southern <a href="/wiki/Bhutan" title="Bhutan">Bhutan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a> and north <a href="/wiki/Indochina" class="mw-redirect" title="Indochina">Indochina</a> (recorded across Chinese border in southwestern <a href="/wiki/Yunnan" title="Yunnan">Yunnan</a>) and spottily in <a href="/wiki/East_China" title="East China">east China</a> where wildlands still occur. Occasionally, wintering birds are known to occur in the central <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a> and discontinuously in <a href="/wiki/Laos" title="Laos">Laos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a> as well as in the <a href="/wiki/Korean_peninsula" class="mw-redirect" title="Korean peninsula">Korean peninsula</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a> and southern <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> (mostly <a href="/wiki/Honshu" title="Honshu">Honshu</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-iucn_1-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iucn-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HBW_4-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Vagrants have been reported in over 20 countries, mainly in Europe, including <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a> and <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Habitat">Habitat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_imperial_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Habitat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eastern_Imperial_Eagle_landscape.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Eastern_Imperial_Eagle_landscape.jpg/220px-Eastern_Imperial_Eagle_landscape.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Eastern_Imperial_Eagle_landscape.jpg/330px-Eastern_Imperial_Eagle_landscape.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Eastern_Imperial_Eagle_landscape.jpg/440px-Eastern_Imperial_Eagle_landscape.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1920" data-file-height="1280" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eastern_Imperial_Eagle_landscape.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>When wintering eastern imperial eagles are partial to relatively open habitats.</div></div></div>
<p>The eastern imperial eagle is distributed as a breeding species largely along the southern edge of the vast <a href="/wiki/Taiga" title="Taiga">taiga</a> forests. The habitat preferred by the species is often rather open country with scattered trees or more enclosed woodlands, often around or near <a href="/wiki/Wetland" title="Wetland">wetlands</a>. The imperial eagle often forages mainly in open areas, including wetlands and <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agricultural areas</a>. The central part of their range as a breeding species occurs in vast areas of <a href="/wiki/Steppe" title="Steppe">steppe</a> and here the species often inhabits <a href="/wiki/Landscape_ecology" title="Landscape ecology">forest-steppe mosaics</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Woodland" title="Woodland">open woodlands</a>, <a href="/wiki/Riparian_zone" title="Riparian zone">river valleys</a> and even agricultural areas with trees or wooded patches.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sanchez-Zapata_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sanchez-Zapata-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fefelov_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fefelov-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Turkestan" title="Turkestan">Turkestan</a> and Kazakhstan, they may extend their breeding habitat into <a href="/wiki/Semi-arid_climate" title="Semi-arid climate">semi-desert</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Largely where golden eagles are absent, the species has been reported to range into secondary habitats such as forested lower <a href="/wiki/Mountain" title="Mountain">mountains</a> and <a href="/wiki/Montane_ecosystems" title="Montane ecosystems">montane steppe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Meadow" title="Meadow">meadowland</a>. In Europe at least, this is due to human pressures, which caused them to abandon open <a href="/wiki/Lowland" class="mw-redirect" title="Lowland">lowlands</a>, timbered <a href="/wiki/Plain" title="Plain">plains</a> and river-fringed forests for forested, precipitous uplands. A slow repopulation of their preferred lowlands reported since 1990s in <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">Slovakia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Horvath3_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horvath3-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Often, eastern imperial eagles winter in more open habitats such as <a href="/wiki/Grassland" title="Grassland">grassland</a>, plains, <a href="/wiki/Semi-arid_climate" title="Semi-arid climate">semi-desert</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Argiculture&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Argiculture (page does not exist)">cultivation</a> with scattered trees as well as various <a href="/wiki/Marsh" title="Marsh">marshes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lake" title="Lake">lakes</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Wetland" title="Wetland">wetlands</a>. The species resides mainly from sea level to 1,300&#160;m (4,300&#160;ft), locally to 1,800&#160;m (5,900&#160;ft), and has been recorded on passage at 3,900&#160;m (12,800&#160;ft) in Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Dietary_biology">Dietary biology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_imperial_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Dietary biology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aquila_heliaca_vs._Eudorcas_thomsonii.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Aquila_heliaca_vs._Eudorcas_thomsonii.JPG/220px-Aquila_heliaca_vs._Eudorcas_thomsonii.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="131" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Aquila_heliaca_vs._Eudorcas_thomsonii.JPG/330px-Aquila_heliaca_vs._Eudorcas_thomsonii.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Aquila_heliaca_vs._Eudorcas_thomsonii.JPG/440px-Aquila_heliaca_vs._Eudorcas_thomsonii.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2684" data-file-height="1596" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aquila_heliaca_vs._Eudorcas_thomsonii.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A juvenile imperial eagle facing off with a <a href="/wiki/Thomson%27s_gazelle" title="Thomson&#39;s gazelle">Thomson's gazelle</a> mother over its dead calf.</div></div></div>
<p>The eastern imperial eagle is somewhat varied in hunting techniques but almost exclusively takes its prey on the ground. It is possible some prey, such as fledgling birds, are taken from low perches in ambushes but this is seemingly unverified. They are also known to capture prey in water or from the edge of waterways and may even become waterlogged, especially when capturing <a href="/wiki/Water_bird" title="Water bird">birds</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Horvath_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horvath-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Imperial eagles typically still hunt, watching for prey at length from a moderately low perch (usually a tree branch but virtually any perch from rocks to bushes to power poles), then often making a short stoop or dive to the ground once prey is spotted. Alternately, they may make a longer dive onto prey from a low soaring flight, often using any vegetation available to obscure their approach.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Some prey are known to be captured on foot, including <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ground_squirrel" title="Ground squirrel">burrowing mammals</a>, the latter reportedly by waiting by the entrance of the animal’s burrow.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Occasionally, this species will pirate foods from other eagles (and other species of bird of prey), especially during winter, and also tandem hunts in pairs as well.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The eastern imperial eagle is, like most active predators, an opportunist who exploits any prey they are capable of overpowering.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Their prey spectrum is highly varied, including somewhere between 200 and 300 prey species, a total number of prey species only a bit short of the occasionally sympatric <a href="/wiki/Common_buzzard" title="Common buzzard">common buzzard</a> (<i>Buteo buteo</i>) which may be 500 times more numerous overall.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Horvath_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horvath-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Katzner_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Katzner-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> In the nations of <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a> alone, the total recorded prey spectrum is 154 and 126 species, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-Horvath_63-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horvath-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Katzner_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Katzner-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Small to medium-sized mammals are the most regularly selected prey, with a preference for <a href="/wiki/Hare" title="Hare">hares</a>, various <a href="/wiki/Rodent" title="Rodent">rodents</a>, especially <a href="/wiki/Ground_squirrel" title="Ground squirrel">ground squirrels</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hamster" title="Hamster">hamsters</a> and  <a href="/wiki/Vole" title="Vole">voles</a>, as well as <a href="/wiki/Eulipotyphla" title="Eulipotyphla">insectivores</a>. Furthermore, various birds are taken, at times as much as or more so than mammals, especially the young or fledglings of various medium-sized to larger birds. Birds may locally become the primary foods in some parts of the winter range. <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">Reptiles</a> are taken secondarily in most of the range but can be locally somewhat important and <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrates</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a>, may be taken rarely.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Horvath_63-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horvath-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The prey type historically most often associated with the eastern imperial eagle, at least in Europe, has been <a href="/wiki/Ground_squirrel" title="Ground squirrel">ground squirrels</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hamster" title="Hamster">hamsters</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> While these are significant, the primary prey type can vary and often <a href="/wiki/Hare" title="Hare">hares</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hedgehog" title="Hedgehog">hedgehogs</a> appear to take the primary position in recent studies.<sup id="cite_ref-Horvath_63-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horvath-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> The largest European dietary study to date, a multi-year analysis from <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a>, showed that <a href="/wiki/European_hare" title="European hare">European hare</a> (<i>Lepus europaeus</i>) were the primary foods, making up 27.4% of a total of 8,543 prey items. The second best represented prey in Hungary was the <a href="/wiki/European_hamster" title="European hamster">European hamster</a> (<i>Cricetus cricetus</i>), at 12.71% of the diet.<sup id="cite_ref-Horvath_63-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horvath-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Horvath2_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horvath2-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, in <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">Slovakia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a>, the European hare was the main prey, comprising 40.2% of 562 prey items and 41.3% of 109 prey items, respectively. The European hamster was the 4th most frequent prey in Slovakia but second most common prey species in the Czech Republic.<sup id="cite_ref-Chavko_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chavko-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Horal_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horal-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> As presented in the multi-year studies from Hungary, a seeming decline of the <a href="/wiki/European_ground_squirrel" title="European ground squirrel">European ground squirrel</a> (<i>Spermophilus citellus</i>) population is the cause of their reduced importance in the imperial eagle’s diet, with this species being the primary prey in 1975-1991 (51% of 606 prey items from 1975 to 1985) to contributing almost nothing in 2005-2017.<sup id="cite_ref-Horvath_63-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horvath-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Horvath2_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horvath2-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Haraszthy_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haraszthy-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> It is possible with reintroductions of the European ground squirrel underway in <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">central Europe</a>, that this prey species may again become more significant in the eastern imperial eagle’s diet again here.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Tuva" title="Tuva">Tyva Republic</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Long-tailed_ground_squirrel" title="Long-tailed ground squirrel">long-tailed ground squirrel</a> (<i>Spermophilus undulatus</i>) still dominates the food of imperial eagles, making up 60.1% of 168 prey items.<sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin2_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin2-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>  In studies of two different areas of <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a>, one showed European hares as the main prey (25%) in the Dervent heights and <a href="/wiki/Southern_white-breasted_hedgehog" title="Southern white-breasted hedgehog">southern white-breasted hedgehog</a> (<i>Erinaceus concolor</i>) (32.5%) in the other, Saker mountain.<sup id="cite_ref-Marin_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marin-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/East_Thrace" title="East Thrace">East Thrace</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, the same hedgehog was the most important prey, comprising 23.1% of 582 prey items and 21.2% of the prey biomass.<sup id="cite_ref-Demerdzhiev_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Demerdzhiev-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Europ%C3%A4ischer_Ziesel_(Spermophilus_citellus)_Schloss_Orth_Nationalparkzentrum_9.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Europ%C3%A4ischer_Ziesel_%28Spermophilus_citellus%29_Schloss_Orth_Nationalparkzentrum_9.jpg/220px-Europ%C3%A4ischer_Ziesel_%28Spermophilus_citellus%29_Schloss_Orth_Nationalparkzentrum_9.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Europ%C3%A4ischer_Ziesel_%28Spermophilus_citellus%29_Schloss_Orth_Nationalparkzentrum_9.jpg/330px-Europ%C3%A4ischer_Ziesel_%28Spermophilus_citellus%29_Schloss_Orth_Nationalparkzentrum_9.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Europ%C3%A4ischer_Ziesel_%28Spermophilus_citellus%29_Schloss_Orth_Nationalparkzentrum_9.jpg/440px-Europ%C3%A4ischer_Ziesel_%28Spermophilus_citellus%29_Schloss_Orth_Nationalparkzentrum_9.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2700" data-file-height="1800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Europ%C3%A4ischer_Ziesel_(Spermophilus_citellus)_Schloss_Orth_Nationalparkzentrum_9.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/European_ground_squirrel" title="European ground squirrel">European ground squirrels</a> are an important food source for eastern imperial eagles.</div></div></div>
<p>In warmer, southerly areas, the primary prey species seem to generally be smaller. In <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Social_vole" title="Social vole">social voles</a> (<i>Microtus socialis</i>) were the primary food, comprising about 15% of 341 prey items.<sup id="cite_ref-Abuladze_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abuladze-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> For wintering imperial eagles, the most frequent live prey (though carrion was mainly eaten) was <a href="/wiki/Sundevall%27s_jird" title="Sundevall&#39;s jird">Sundevall's jird</a> (<i>Meriones crassus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Hasani_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hasani-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Both of these small rodents probably average only about 35 to 60&#160;g (1.2 to 2.1&#160;oz) in body mass.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Numerous other small mammals may also be occasionally taken including several species each of <a href="/wiki/Hedgehog" title="Hedgehog">hedgehogs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shrew" title="Shrew">shrews</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mole_(animal)" title="Mole (animal)">moles</a>, beyond the common hare, at least 7 other species of <a href="/wiki/Lagomorph" class="mw-redirect" title="Lagomorph">lagomorphs</a>, about a dozen species each of <a href="/wiki/Muridae" title="Muridae">murid rodents</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cricetidae" title="Cricetidae">cricetid rodents</a> (especially <a href="/wiki/Hamster" title="Hamster">hamsters</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vole" title="Vole">voles</a>), 5 species of <a href="/wiki/Spalacidae" title="Spalacidae">zokor</a> and assorted <a href="/wiki/Dormice" class="mw-redirect" title="Dormice">dormice</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jerboa" title="Jerboa">jerboas</a>. Thus, eastern imperial eagles appear to prefer rodents and similar small mammals that are burrow-dwelling and/or partial to ground dwelling in open grass or fields along wooded edges.<sup id="cite_ref-Horvath_63-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horvath-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Chavko_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chavko-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin_36-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Demerdzhiev_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Demerdzhiev-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin2_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin2-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hasani_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hasani-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Zhelev_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zhelev-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Bar-headed_Goose_%28Anser_indicus%29%2C_Imperial_Eagle_%28Aquila_heliaca%29%2C_Northern_Pintail_%28Anas_acuta%29_%26_Great_Cormorant_%28Phalacrocorax_carbo%29_%2832774244330%29.jpg/330px-Bar-headed_Goose_%28Anser_indicus%29%2C_Imperial_Eagle_%28Aquila_heliaca%29%2C_Northern_Pintail_%28Anas_acuta%29_%26_Great_Cormorant_%28Phalacrocorax_carbo%29_%2832774244330%29.jpg title="Enlarge"></a></div>An eastern imperial eagle in the upper left side can be seen hunting flocks of <a href="/wiki/Bar-headed_goose" title="Bar-headed goose">bar-headed goose</a> and <a href="/wiki/Northern_pintail" title="Northern pintail">northern pintail</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Assorted bird species may factor heavily into the diet of eastern imperial eagles. More than 120 bird species are known to be taken by this eagle.<sup id="cite_ref-Horvath_63-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horvath-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Katzner_65-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Katzner-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> European studies reflect the high importance of <a href="/wiki/Common_pheasant" title="Common pheasant">common pheasants</a> (<i>Phasianus colchicus</i>), in Europe a non-native gamebird (although the imperial eagle also encounters and hunts the species extensively in its native range as well), in their foods. In the aforementioned large Hungarian study, pheasants were the third most frequently taken prey species, making up 12% of the diet.<sup id="cite_ref-Horvath_63-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horvath-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Horvath2_67-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horvath2-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> In the Czech Republic, the pheasant ranked third as well.<sup id="cite_ref-Horal_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horal-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> In Slovakia, the pheasant was second only to the hare in frequency, comprising 17.3% of the foods, although exclusively juvenile and hen pheasants (no cocks) were reportedly taken.<sup id="cite_ref-Chavko_68-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chavko-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> In Bulgarian studies, the <a href="/wiki/Chicken" title="Chicken">domestic chicken</a> (<i>Gallus gallus domesticus</i>) seems to take the place of pheasants in their diet, making up 10.8% and 20.8% at Saker mountain and Dervent heights and second most frequently taken prey at both, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-Marin_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marin-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> The largest study of the eastern imperial eagle’s dietary habits known occurred in the vast <a href="/wiki/Naurzum_Nature_Reserve" title="Naurzum Nature Reserve">Naurzum Nature Reserve</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a>, where 11,079 prey items were reviewed. The prey spectrum was exceptionally diverse here, with no one prey reliably being favored by the imperial eagle pairs, despite an ample colony of <a href="/wiki/Yellow_ground_squirrel" title="Yellow ground squirrel">yellow ground squirrel</a> (<i>Spermophilus fulvus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Russet_ground_squirrel" title="Russet ground squirrel">russet ground squirrel</a> (<i>Spermophilus major</i>) being nearby and at least three other species of eagles with nearby nesting sites presenting possible resource competition. Without presenting the metrics, apparently birds were the highest volume prey for the imperial eagles, especially <a href="/wiki/Corvid" class="mw-redirect" title="Corvid">corvids</a>, namely the <a href="/wiki/Rook_(bird)" title="Rook (bird)">rook</a> (<i>Corvus frugilegus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_magpie" title="Eurasian magpie">Eurasian magpie</a> (<i>Pica pica</i>), numerous species of <a href="/wiki/Duck" title="Duck">duck</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_kestrel" class="mw-redirect" title="Eurasian kestrel">Eurasian kestrels</a> (<i>Falco tinnunculus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Little_bustard" title="Little bustard">little bustards</a> (<i>Tetrax tetrax</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Katzner_65-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Katzner-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Katzner2_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Katzner2-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/East_Thrace" title="East Thrace">East Thrace</a>, Turkey, the second most regularly taken prey species is the <a href="/wiki/Yellow-legged_gull" title="Yellow-legged gull">yellow-legged gull</a> (<i>Larus michahellis</i>), which comprised more than 12% of the diet by number and 13.8% of the prey biomass. Additionally in East Thrace, a high volume of <a href="/wiki/White_stork" title="White stork">white storks</a> (<i>Ciconia ciconia</i>) was taken, making up 11.3% of the biomass.<sup id="cite_ref-Demerdzhiev_74-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Demerdzhiev-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> In Bulgaria, similar prey were important secondary foods, i.e. <a href="/wiki/Caspian_gull" title="Caspian gull">Caspian gulls</a> (<i>Larus cachinnans</i>) at 9.78% of the diet in the Saker mountain area, while white stork made up 10.42% of the diet at Dervent heights.<sup id="cite_ref-Marin_73-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marin-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> A study of wintering eastern imperial eagles in the <a href="/wiki/Bharatpur_district" title="Bharatpur district">Bharatpur district</a> of <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> showed that this species was generally more inactive but also more likely to capture its own food (rather than through scavenging or <a href="/wiki/Kleptoparasitism" title="Kleptoparasitism">kleptoparasitism</a>) than 4 assorted other eagle species in the area. Like other eagles here, the imperial eagles most often fed on various <a href="/wiki/Water_bird" title="Water bird">water birds</a>, mainly the nestlings of late-nesting <a href="/wiki/Painted_stork" title="Painted stork">painted storks</a> (<i>Ciconia leucocephalus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Black-headed_ibis" title="Black-headed ibis">black-headed ibis</a> (<i>Threskiornis melanocephalus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Oriental_darter" title="Oriental darter">Oriental darters</a> (<i>Anhinga melanogaster</i>) and a couple of species of <a href="/wiki/Cormorant" title="Cormorant">cormorant</a>. However, the imperial eagle in particularly here took to regularly hunting various adult water birds especially <a href="/wiki/Duck" title="Duck">ducks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greylag_goose" title="Greylag goose">geese</a> and large <a href="/wiki/Rail_(bird)" title="Rail (bird)">rails</a> and had a mean daily food intake (not mean prey size) of 539&#160;g (1.188&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Prakash_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prakash-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Saurashtra_(region)" title="Saurashtra (region)">Saurashtra region</a> of <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, imperial eagles were observed to show a preference for hunting diving water birds, including <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_coot" title="Eurasian coot">Eurasian coots</a> (<i>Fulica atra</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Diving_duck" title="Diving duck">diving ducks</a>, which they would hunt in a style reminiscent of the <a href="/wiki/White-tailed_eagle" title="White-tailed eagle">white-tailed eagle</a>, forcing them to dive as they circled over the water and capturing them as they came to the water’s surface to breathe.<sup id="cite_ref-Dharmakuarsinhji_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dharmakuarsinhji-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Numerous <a href="/wiki/Columbidae" title="Columbidae">pigeons and doves</a> may also be taken fairly often, such as in <a
