href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">Slovakia</a> where <a href="/wiki/Rock_pigeon" class="mw-redirect" title="Rock pigeon">rock pigeons</a> (<i>Columba livia</i>) were the 3rd most often regular prey at 11.79% of the diet.<sup id="cite_ref-Horvath_63-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horvath-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Chavko_68-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chavko-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> In general, a picture emerges of the imperial eagle’s dietary preference for relatively large birds with conspicuous behaviour, relatively slow flight, who can be struck on or near the ground and/or have vulnerable nesting sites or conspicuous young, such as <a href="/wiki/Gamebird" class="mw-redirect" title="Gamebird">gamebirds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Waterfowl" class="mw-redirect" title="Waterfowl">waterfowl</a>, other <a href="/wiki/Water_bird" title="Water bird">water birds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Corvid" class="mw-redirect" title="Corvid">corvids</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Horvath_63-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horvath-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Katzner_65-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Katzner-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Katzner2_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Katzner2-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>European studies of the eastern imperial eagle’s diet rarely reflect prey outside of the main preferred classes of mammals and birds, however studies from somewhat outside Europe show respectable numbers of <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">reptiles</a> may be taken. In <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a>, reptiles amounted to 29.62% of the food, comprised largely either of <a href="/wiki/Caucasian_agama" title="Caucasian agama">Caucasian agama</a> (<i>Paralaudakia caucasia</i>) or other unidentified smallish lizards.<sup id="cite_ref-Abuladze_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abuladze-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> A larger class of reptiles were regular secondary prey in East Thrace in Turkey, namely <a href="/wiki/Greek_tortoise" title="Greek tortoise">Greek tortoise</a> (<i>Testudo graeca</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Hermann%27s_tortoise" title="Hermann&#39;s tortoise">Hermann's tortoise</a> (<i>Testudo hermanni</i>), with tortoises altogether comprising 11.1% of the diet by number and 13.7% of the prey biomass.<sup id="cite_ref-Demerdzhiev_74-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Demerdzhiev-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Tortoises can be significant in the diet elsewhere as well, especially in more arid climes.<sup id="cite_ref-Hasani_76-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hasani-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Although <a href="/wiki/Snake" title="Snake">snakes</a> do not seem to be typically quantitatively important, eastern imperial eagles have no problem occasionally subduing large snakes, such as <a href="/wiki/Aesculapian_snake" title="Aesculapian snake">Aesculapian snakes</a> (<i>Zamenis longissimus</i>), or very aggressive venomous snakes, such as <a href="/wiki/Russell%27s_viper" title="Russell&#39;s viper">Russell's viper</a> (<i>Daboia russelii</i>) (the latter taken in their wintering Indian quarters).<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Horvath_63-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horvath-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a>, very small numbers of <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrates</a> (mostly <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Ground_beetle" title="Ground beetle">ground beetles</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a> were found amongst the foods of imperial eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-Horvath_63-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horvath-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Carrion is eaten through the year by eastern imperial eagles, but most heavily during winter.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> For example, in the wintering population of <a href="/wiki/Jordan" title="Jordan">Jordan</a>, 53.7% of the dietary intake of the species was made up of carrion.<sup id="cite_ref-Hasani_76-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hasani-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> However, in some breeding populations, apparently the eagles can come to rely on dead or already injured prey inadvertently provided by humans, largely due to intensive agricultural practices, as was the case in the <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Horal_69-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horal-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> In one area of <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">Slovakia</a>, although imperial eagles also hunted, the adult eagles routinely practiced <a href="/wiki/Kleptoparasitism" title="Kleptoparasitism">kleptoparasitism</a> while nesting, regularly robbing other species of raptorial bird of their fresh catches.<sup id="cite_ref-Danko_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Danko-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Almost any mammal or bird will be readily eaten when dead or dying by imperial eagles, with at least 10 species of <a href="/wiki/Ungulate" title="Ungulate">ungulate</a> known to be consumed thusly and providing an ample source of meat.<sup id="cite_ref-Horvath_63-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horvath-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Chavko_68-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chavko-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marin_73-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marin-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The size of prey taken by eastern imperial eagles can be fairly variable, though is not generally as impressively varying as the golden eagle’s prey sizes.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Katzner_65-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Katzner-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> Most live prey taken by eastern imperial eagles weighs less than 2&#160;kg (4.4&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Many of the prey species taken by imperial eagles are relatively large as adults such as <a href="/wiki/European_hare" title="European hare">European hares</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bobak_marmot" title="Bobak marmot">bobak marmots</a> (<i>Marmota bobac</i>), which were the second most often taken mammalian prey species in Kazakhstan, but generally, these eagles take juvenile specimens of both hares and marmots rather than prime adults. The mean weight of <a href="/wiki/European_hare" title="European hare">European hares</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bobak_marmot" title="Bobak marmot">bobak marmots</a> taken in Kazakhstan was estimated at 1.5&#160;kg (3.3&#160;lb) and 1.4&#160;kg (3.1&#160;lb), in both cases no less than a third of the average adult weight attainable by the species. In contrast, the smallest staple prey in Kazakhstan were small rodents (i.e. mice, voles) that weighed an estimated 30&#160;g (1.1&#160;oz).<sup id="cite_ref-Katzner_65-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Katzner-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Katzner2_80-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Katzner2-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, the upper size of hare typically taken in Slovakia was estimated at 1.4&#160;kg (3.1&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Chavko_68-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chavko-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> In Hungary, staple prey size was estimated at between 250&#160;g (8.8&#160;oz) and 2.5&#160;kg (5.5&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Horvath_63-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horvath-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> However, the eastern imperial eagle is capable of taking large, impressive prey as well. In some cases, they are capable of taking adult hares, including the typical European hares but also other species such as <a href="/wiki/Mountain_hare" title="Mountain hare">mountain hare</a> (<i>Lepus timidus</i>) and the <a href="/wiki/Tolai_hare" title="Tolai hare">Tolai hare</a> (<i>Lepus tolai</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Horvath_63-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horvath-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin2_72-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin2-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ryabtsev_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ryabtsev-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> They have taken adults of numerous larger water birds averaging over the expected prey weight of 2&#160;kg (4.4&#160;lb), although (at least for wading birds such as stork) nestlings are most often preyed upon, including <a href="/wiki/Greylag_goose" title="Greylag goose">greylag goose</a> (<i>Anser anser</i>), <a href="/wiki/Greater_white-fronted_goose" title="Greater white-fronted goose">greater white-fronted goose</a> (<i>Anser albifrons</i>), <a href="/wiki/Bean_goose" title="Bean goose">bean goose</a> (<i>Anser fabalis</i>), <a href="/wiki/Bar-headed_goose" title="Bar-headed goose">bar-headed goose</a> (<i>Anser indicus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Knob-billed_duck" title="Knob-billed duck">knob-billed duck</a> (<i>Sarkidiornis melanotos</i>), <a href="/wiki/Common_crane" title="Common crane">common crane</a> (<i>Grus grus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Great_cormorant" title="Great cormorant">great cormorant</a> (<i>Phalacrocorax carbo</i>), <a href="/wiki/White_stork" title="White stork">white stork</a> and <a href="/wiki/Black_stork" title="Black stork">black stork</a> (<i>Ciconia nigra</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-HBW_4-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HBW-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Horvath_63-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horvath-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marin_73-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marin-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Altai_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Altai-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Eastern imperial eagles also attack the young of <a href="/wiki/Ungulate" title="Ungulate">ungulates</a> at times, reportedly neonatal and mildly older calves and lambs of similar size to the eagles themselves, including species such as <a href="/wiki/Argali" title="Argali">argali</a> (<i>Ovis ammon</i>), <a href="/wiki/Roe_deer" title="Roe deer">roe deer</a> (<i>Capreolus capreolus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Arabian_sand_gazelle" title="Arabian sand gazelle">Arabian sand gazelle</a> (<i>Gazella marica</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Goitered_gazelle" title="Goitered gazelle">goitered gazelle</a> (<i>Gazella subgutturosa</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Chavko_68-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chavko-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> At the opposite end of the scale in vertebrate prey, imperial eagles are known to take mammals down to the size of the 7&#160;g (0.25&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_harvest_mouse" title="Eurasian harvest mouse">Eurasian harvest mouse</a> (<i>Micromys minutus</i>) and birds down to the size of the 21.4&#160;g (0.75&#160;oz) <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_tree_sparrow" title="Eurasian tree sparrow">Eurasian tree sparrow</a> (<i>Passer montanus</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-CRC_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CRC-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Horvath_63-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horvath-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> In Kazakhstan, the estimated size of all prey taken varied from approximately 2&#160;g (0.071&#160;oz) for a <a href="/wiki/Locust" title="Locust">locust</a> to 5&#160;kg (11&#160;lb) for the largest bird prey (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Common_crane" title="Common crane">cranes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greylag_goose" title="Greylag goose">geese</a>) and 5.7&#160;kg (13&#160;lb) for the largest mammals taken (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Bobak_marmot" title="Bobak marmot">adult marmots</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sheep" title="Sheep">domestic lambs</a> (<i>Ovis aries</i>)).<sup id="cite_ref-Katzner_65-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Katzner-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Katzner2_80-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Katzner2-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> It is known that eastern imperial eagles will also prey upon <a href="/wiki/Great_bustard" title="Great bustard">great bustards</a> (<i>Otis tarda</i>), though the female is possibly taken as she is about as large as a crane or large goose, it is unlikely that the eagle can take the much larger adult males of this huge ground bird, since imperial eagles apparently even avoids adult male birds of much smaller species such as <a href="/wiki/Common_pheasant" title="Common pheasant">pheasants</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Horvath_63-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horvath-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Chavko_68-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chavko-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Eastern imperial eagles select broadly overlapping prey with <a href="/wiki/Carnivora" title="Carnivora">mammalian carnivorans</a> but also fairly regularly attack these as prey. <a href="/wiki/Fox" title="Fox">Foxes</a> are widely known in the foods of imperial eagles but at times at visited as carrion. Most attacks on <a href="/wiki/Red_fox" title="Red fox">red foxes</a> (<i>Vulpes vulpes</i>) are on juvenile, other than perhaps small females, adults at average weights of up to 6 to 10&#160;kg (13 to 22&#160;lb) in some areas are probably too large for this eagle to overpower. However attacks and successful predation has been committed on adult <a href="/wiki/Corsac_fox" title="Corsac fox">corsac foxes</a> (<i>Vulpes corsac</i>) as well as probably <a href="/wiki/Bengal_fox" title="Bengal fox">bengal foxes</a> (<i>Vulpes bengalensis</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Katzner_65-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Katzner-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marin_73-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marin-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Katzner2_80-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Katzner2-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ellis_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Beyond small <a href="/wiki/Weasel" title="Weasel">weasels</a>, which are probably no issue for large eagles to attack, larger <a href="/wiki/Mustelid" class="mw-redirect" title="Mustelid">mustelids</a> may too be attacked including <a href="/wiki/European_polecat" title="European polecat">European</a> (<i>Mustela putorius</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Steppe_polecat" title="Steppe polecat">steppe polecats</a> (<i>Mustela eversmanii</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Stone_marten" class="mw-redirect" title="Stone marten">stone martens</a> (<i>Martes foina</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-Horvath_63-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horvath-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Small or kitten <a href="/wiki/Cat" title="Cat">domestic cats</a> (<i>Felis silvestris catus</i>) are sometimes prey for eastern imperial eagles and juvenile <a href="/wiki/Pallas%27s_cat" title="Pallas&#39;s cat">Pallas's cats</a> (<i>Otocolobus manul</i>) may too be vulnerable to this eagle.<sup id="cite_ref-Horvath_63-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horvath-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Chavko_68-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chavko-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interspecies_predatory_relationships">Interspecies predatory relationships</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_imperial_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Interspecies predatory relationships">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The eastern imperial eagle is a powerful bird of prey but frequently overlaps in range with multiple other eagle species and that may require them to share both habitats and prey.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Golden_eagle" title="Golden eagle">golden eagle</a> is generally a larger, more powerful bird. It is also more tends to be a bolder, more aggressive predator than the imperial eagle and may be able to attack much larger prey. While the mean prey body mass relative to the eagle’s weight is probably similar between the two species, an estimated 15% of golden eagle prey will weigh over 5&#160;kg (11&#160;lb).<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ellis_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ellis-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> In its very extensive range, the golden eagle’s distribution includes nearly all areas occupied by breeding eastern imperial eagles. Furthermore, there is considerable overlap in prey species selected by these species.<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_6-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Katzner3_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Katzner3-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vazhov_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vazhov-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> There is a natural partitioning between the two <i>Aquila</i> species and that comes in the form of habitat preferences. The golden eagle takes to, usually but not always, rocky and uneven terrain, so favors mountainous areas with alpine meadows to access for prey. This is quite different from the eastern imperial eagle preference for flat or somewhat rolling interface between wood stands and fields at low elevations.<sup id="cite_ref-Katzner4_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Katzner4-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Marti_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marti-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> However, in some areas, especially eastern Europe, eastern imperial eagles have been driven to higher elevations and more montane habitats that are typically the haunts of golden eagles by persecution, habitat destruction and other interferences by humans, usually with mixed to minor success as the golden species is scarce at best locally and unlikely to produce competition.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Horvath3_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horvath3-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> There is surprisingly little information on conflicts between these two eagle species.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Naurzum_Nature_Reserve" title="Naurzum Nature Reserve">Naurzum Nature Reserve</a> of <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a> the golden and eastern imperial eagle, and to some extent also both <a href="/wiki/White-tailed_eagle" title="White-tailed eagle">white-tailed eagles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Steppe_eagle" title="Steppe eagle">steppe eagles</a>, were recorded nesting with a fairly close proximity to each other. The eagle species here would even use nests built by the other species and seemed to have similar or broadly overlapping food habits, but no interspecies conflicts were detected.<sup id="cite_ref-Katzner3_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Katzner3-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> On the contrary, in the <a href="/wiki/Altai_Republic" title="Altai Republic">Altai Republic</a>, it appears that golden and eastern imperial eagles are considered to fill a largely similar <a href="/wiki/Ecological_niche" title="Ecological niche">ecological niche</a> in abutting areas and do compete for nesting sites.<sup id="cite_ref-Altai_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Altai-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Vazhov_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vazhov-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> In some cases in Europe, golden and eastern imperial eagles will engage in a territorial display if prompted against one another.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eastern_Imperial_Eagle_(24979452083).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Eastern_Imperial_Eagle_%2824979452083%29.jpg/220px-Eastern_Imperial_Eagle_%2824979452083%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="326" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Eastern_Imperial_Eagle_%2824979452083%29.jpg/330px-Eastern_Imperial_Eagle_%2824979452083%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Eastern_Imperial_Eagle_%2824979452083%29.jpg/440px-Eastern_Imperial_Eagle_%2824979452083%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="691" data-file-height="1024" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eastern_Imperial_Eagle_(24979452083).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Eastern imperial eagles may be attracted to carrion quite often especially in winter. Here with another scavenger, the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_vulture" title="Egyptian vulture">Egyptian vulture</a>, in the background.</div></div></div>
<p>Of a similar distribution to eastern imperial eagles, both in their mid-Eurasian breeding ground and southerly Indo-African wintering grounds, are the <a href="/wiki/Steppe_eagle" title="Steppe eagle">steppe eagle</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Greater_spotted_eagle" title="Greater spotted eagle">greater spotted eagle</a>. There is a fair amount of habitat partitioning between the three species, however, with the steppe eagle preferring flat, often almost treeless <a href="/wiki/Steppe" title="Steppe">steppe</a> while the greater spotted eagle prefers more densely wooded and wetter habitats generally than imperial eagles such as <a href="/wiki/Taiga" title="Taiga">taiga</a> <a href="/wiki/Bog" title="Bog">bogs</a>. Diet however can overlap considerably, especially with steppe eagles as both it and the imperial species are largely attracted to colonies of <a href="/wiki/Ground_squirrel" title="Ground squirrel">ground squirrels</a> in <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> While steppe eagles occur much more broadly in Africa during winter and a similarly narrow extent in the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a> as eastern imperials, greater spotted eagles are similarly as rare as imperial eagles in Africa but spread farther in Asia than either the steppe or imperial species. In all three species, by winter they are attracted to more open habitats ranging from <a href="/wiki/Savanna" title="Savanna">savanna</a> to <a href="/wiki/Wetland" title="Wetland">wetlands</a> and even <a href="/wiki/Desert" title="Desert">semi-desert</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-iucn_1-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iucn-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The ecology of wintering eastern imperials was studied at length in <a href="/wiki/Bharatpur_district" title="Bharatpur district">Bharatpur district</a> of <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> in contrast to the steppe and greater spotted eagle as well as the resident <a href="/wiki/Indian_spotted_eagle" title="Indian spotted eagle">Indian spotted eagle</a> and shorter-distance migrant <a href="/wiki/Pallas%27s_fish_eagle" title="Pallas&#39;s fish eagle">Pallas's fish eagle</a>. It was found that the feeding opportunities sought were largely similar (nestling water birds were often favored) by all five eagle species and that a hierarchy was formed, though each species competed most regularly with others of their own species. The eastern imperial eagle was, by and large, dominant in correspondence to its slightly larger size than the other booted eagles and rivaled the similarly-sized Pallas's fish eagle as the top avian predator in this raptor community. The steppe eagle, despite being only scarcely smaller than an imperial eagle, was usually subordinate to imperial eagles and had a much lower estimated average daily food intake, 141&#160;g (5.0&#160;oz) against an average of 539&#160;g (1.188&#160;lb) for the imperial. However, the mean daily intake of Pallas's fish eagle was slightly higher still at 623&#160;g (1.373&#160;lb) and the fish eagle would perch slightly higher than the imperials as well. In one case, a flock of 9 steppe eagles was able to pirate a freshly caught <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_coot" title="Eurasian coot">coot</a> from an imperial eagle. The imperial was the most inactive forager here, having spent 36% of observed hours foraging, against 45% for steppe eagles, 46% for Pallas's fish eagle, 49% for greater spotted eagle and 65% for Indian spotted eagle.<sup id="cite_ref-Naoroji_24-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Naoroji-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Prakash_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prakash-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> Other studies on the interactions of eastern imperial eagles also support that it is dominant during winter over similar species such as steppe and the spotted eagles at competitive feeding spots.<sup id="cite_ref-Dharmakuarsinhji_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dharmakuarsinhji-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Korean_peninsula" class="mw-redirect" title="Korean peninsula">Korean peninsula</a>, similarly as in the Indian subcontinent, wintering eastern imperial eagles (though rare) can reportedly be seen more than singly concentrated where there are ample numbers of <a href="/wiki/Water_bird" title="Water bird">water birds</a> along with other large eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Next to nothing is known about the ecology of the rare, seldom-observed wintering population of eastern imperial eagles in <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">east Africa</a> but it is claimed to usually be seen in the company of “other brown eagles”.<sup id="cite_ref-Kemp_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kemp-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As its preferred habitat seldom overlaps with larger eagles such as golden eagles and <a href="/wiki/White-tailed_eagle" title="White-tailed eagle">white-tailed eagles</a>, the eastern imperial eagle is usually the top avian predator in its breeding grounds. In particular, smaller raptors with largely overlapping diets (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Ground_squirrel" title="Ground squirrel">ground squirrels</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hamster" title="Hamster">hamsters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vole" title="Vole">voles</a> &amp; <a href="/wiki/Lagomorph" class="mw-redirect" title="Lagomorph">lagomorphs</a>) and habitat preferences such as <a href="/wiki/Saker_falcon" title="Saker falcon">saker falcons</a> (<i>Falco cherrug</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Long-legged_buzzard" title="Long-legged buzzard">long-legged buzzards</a> (<i>Buteo rufinus</i>) are often at a disadvantage in direct competition with the eagle species.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">Slovakia</a>, some pairs of imperial eagle were reported to <a href="/wiki/Kleptoparasitism" title="Kleptoparasitism">kleptoparasitize</a> other raptors as a matter of routine. Here, four species consisting of <a href="/wiki/Saker_falcon" title="Saker falcon">saker falcons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Western_marsh_harrier" title="Western marsh harrier">western marsh harrier</a> (<i>Circus aeruginosus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Black-winged_kite" title="Black-winged kite">black-winged kite</a> (<i>Elanus caeruleus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_sparrowhawk" title="Eurasian sparrowhawk">Eurasian sparrowhawk</a> (<i>Accipiter nisus</i>) as well as <a href="/wiki/Red_fox" title="Red fox">red foxes</a> were all robbed of their catches with a remarkable degree of success by imperial eagles. The eagles so heavily depleted the falcons catches that the falcons’ nesting attempts failed.<sup id="cite_ref-Danko_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Danko-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> A still swifter falcon than the saker, the <a href="/wiki/Peregrine_falcon" title="Peregrine falcon">peregrine falcon</a> (<i>Falco peregrinus</i>) was observed successfully robbing imperial eagles and a few other raptorial birds several times when the species nested near each other in the lower <a href="/wiki/Sakmara_river" class="mw-redirect" title="Sakmara river">Sakmara river</a> of <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> (although in one case, a juvenile peregrine was killed by the golden eagle it was attempting to rob). Interestingly, many of the imperial eagles kills that were robbed by the peregrines were other species of bird of prey.<sup id="cite_ref-Moshkin_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moshkin-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Tartarstan" class="mw-redirect" title="Tartarstan">Tartarstan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> it was found that eastern imperial eagles have begun nesting in atypical habitats and locations, namely the old nests of white-tailed eagles in isolated trees amongst open wetlands and old nests of greater spotted eagle in densely wooded bogs. Despite the remaining presence of both other eagle species in the area, competition is probably not the driver for the imperial eagle altering its nesting habits but instead it is likely due to the heavy human-caused depletion of the imperial eagles preferred prey of ground squirrels and hamsters in the area, with the wetland-located nests putting them close to currently reliable alternate primary foods, mainly water birds.<sup id="cite_ref-Bekmansurov_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bekmansurov-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The eastern imperial eagle may be characterized as an occasional and opportunistic predator of other birds of prey. The following raptorial birds have been known to fall prey this eagle: the <a href="/wiki/Lesser_spotted_eagle" title="Lesser spotted eagle">lesser spotted eagle</a> (<i>Clanga pomarina</i>), <a href="/wiki/European_honey_buzzard" title="European honey buzzard">European honey buzzard</a> (<i>Pernis apivorus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Black_kite" title="Black kite">black kite</a> (<i>Milvus migrans</i>), <a href="/wiki/Hen_harrier" title="Hen harrier">hen harrier</a> (<i>Circus cyaneus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Montagu%27s_harrier" title="Montagu&#39;s harrier">Montagu's harrier</a> (<i>Circus pygargus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Western_marsh_harrier" title="Western marsh harrier">western marsh harrier</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_sparrowhawk" title="Eurasian sparrowhawk">Eurasian sparrowhawk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Northern_goshawk" title="Northern goshawk">northern goshawk</a> (<i>Accipiter gentilis</i>), <a href="/wiki/Common_buzzard" title="Common buzzard">common buzzard</a> (<i>Buteo buteo</i>), <a href="/wiki/Long-legged_buzzard" title="Long-legged buzzard">long-legged buzzard</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rough-legged_buzzard" title="Rough-legged buzzard">rough-legged buzzard</a> (<i>Buteo lagopus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Ural_owl" title="Ural owl">Ural owl</a> (<i>Strix uralensis</i>), <a href="/wiki/Tawny_owl" title="Tawny owl">tawny owl</a> (<i>Strix alucco</i>), <a href="/wiki/Little_owl" title="Little owl">little owl</a> (<i>Athene noctua</i>), <a href="/wiki/Long-eared_owl" title="Long-eared owl">long-eared owl</a> (<i>Asio otus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Short-eared_owl" title="Short-eared owl">short-eared owl</a> (<i>Asio flammeus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_hobby" title="Eurasian hobby">Eurasian hobby</a> (<i>Falco subbuteo</i>), <a href="/wiki/Common_kestrel" title="Common kestrel">common kestrel</a> (<i>Falco tinnunculus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Lesser_kestrel" title="Lesser kestrel">lesser kestrel</a> (<i>Falco naumanni</i>), <a href="/wiki/Merlin_(bird)" title="Merlin (bird)">merlin</a> (<i>Falco columbarius</i>), <a href="/wiki/Red-footed_falcon" title="Red-footed falcon">red-footed falcon</a> (<i>Falco vespertinus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Saker_falcon" title="Saker falcon">saker falcon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Peregrine_falcon" title="Peregrine falcon">peregrine falcon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Horvath_63-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horvath-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Katzner_65-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Katzner-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Chavko_68-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chavko-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Demerdzhiev_74-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Demerdzhiev-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hasani_76-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hasani-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Katzner2_80-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Katzner2-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Moshkin_112-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moshkin-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Korepov_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Korepov-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Adamian_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adamian-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> Although it may usually be classed as an <a href="/wiki/Apex_predator" title="Apex predator">apex predator</a>, eastern imperial eagles have fallen prey to other birds of prey on rare occasions. An instance of predation was reportedly committed by a <a href="/wiki/White-tailed_eagle" title="White-tailed eagle">white-tailed eagle</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Palmer_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Palmer-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> Also, an imperial eagle was killed by an <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_eagle-owl" title="Eurasian eagle-owl">Eurasian eagle-owl</a> (<i>Bubo bubo</i>) in Kazakhstan. This very large owl is a “super predator” which broadly overlaps in prey selection with imperial eagles (at least in the <a href="/wiki/Altai_Republic" title="Altai Republic">Altai Republic</a>). However, the eagle-owl also threatens nearly all birds of prey in their range with nighttime ambushes (even golden eagles).<sup id="cite_ref-Watson_6-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Breeding">Breeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_imperial_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Breeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aquila_heliaca_nest.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Aquila_heliaca_nest.jpg/220px-Aquila_heliaca_nest.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="308" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Aquila_heliaca_nest.jpg/330px-Aquila_heliaca_nest.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Aquila_heliaca_nest.jpg/440px-Aquila_heliaca_nest.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="700" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aquila_heliaca_nest.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nest of eastern imperial eagle in <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The eastern imperial eagle is a fairly solitary bird, with rarely more than a pair occurring, although some small gatherings recorded at waterholes or food during migrant or winter. Pairs on the breeding grounds engage in aerial displays with loud calling and extensive high circling, which like most of accipitrids is likely for the purposes territorial proclamation to other eagles of their species. The display is often interspersed with mock dives and talon showing. One or both members of a pair may participate in the aerial display. If an interloper does not leave during the first part of the display, the territorial skirmish then may become physical and it sometimes leads to cartwheeling with interlocked talons, falling until they nearly hit the ground. Despite the fairly impressive display, it is generally of slightly shorter duration than the similar one done by <a href="/wiki/Golden_eagle" title="Golden eagle">golden eagles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/East_Thrace" title="East Thrace">East Thrace</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, the mean nearest nest distance between actively breeding pairs was 10.44&#160;km (6.49&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-Demerdzhiev_74-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Demerdzhiev-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Demerdzhiev2_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Demerdzhiev2-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Irkutsk_Oblast" title="Irkutsk Oblast">Irkutsk Oblast</a> near <a href="/wiki/Lake_Baikal" title="Lake Baikal">Lake Baikal</a>, the mean distance of the central area of nests (each pair had more than one nest on territory) was 17&#160;km (11&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-Fefelov_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fefelov-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> The breeding season lasts from late March to September throughout the range but in their former range in <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> (where it is now extirpated as a breeder) was said to extend from November to April.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Khan_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Khan-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> The pair constructs a very large nest of sticks, which may average up to 1.2 to 1.5&#160;m (3&#160;ft 11&#160;in to 4&#160;ft 11&#160;in) across and 60 to 70&#160;cm (24 to 28&#160;in) in depth. However, nests for the species have been recorded to measure up to 2.4&#160;m (7&#160;ft 10&#160;in) or more across and 1.8 to 2&#160;m (5&#160;ft 11&#160;in to 6&#160;ft 7&#160;in) deep. Nests can outmatch in size those even of larger species such as golden eagles.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fefelov_60-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fefelov-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Nests are frequently lined with various materials, including twigs, grass, fur, debris and greenery. Usually eastern imperial eagles build their nest in tall trees but recorded down to as low as 2&#160;m (6.6&#160;ft) in low scrub or, rarely, on cliff or ground (later as recorded in the <a href="/wiki/Sayan_mountains" class="mw-redirect" title="Sayan mountains">Sayan mountains</a> of Kazakhstan).<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Nest sites have been found anywhere from in the depth of a forest to forest edge to a solitary tree that may be visible for miles in open plains.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Nesting sites in both Turkey and Georgia were consistently under 450&#160;m (1,480&#160;ft) elevation. In Georgia, denser mixed forests with openings were favored alternately with more arid, isolated stands of <a href="/wiki/Juniper" title="Juniper">juniper</a> (<i>Juniperus ssp</i>.) and <a href="/wiki/Pistachio" title="Pistachio">pistachio</a> (<i>Pistacia vera</i>); in Turkey, <a href="/wiki/Populus" title="Populus">poplar</a> (<i>Populus ssp</i>.) (44%) and <a href="/wiki/Quercus" class="mw-redirect" title="Quercus">oak</a> (<i>Quercus ssp</i>.) (40%) were favored.<sup id="cite_ref-Abuladze_75-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abuladze-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Demerdzhiev2_121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Demerdzhiev2-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> In extensive Russian studies, 78% of nests were in <a href="/wiki/Conifer" class="mw-redirect" title="Conifer">conifers</a> when compared with Kazakhstan where deciduous trees are more prevalent, however in the latter country conifers are still used where they are found.<sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin_36-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Unusual nest sites in agricultural land of <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">Slovakia</a> include one next to a <a href="/wiki/Railroad" class="mw-redirect" title="Railroad">railroad</a> and another next to a busy road.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> The eastern imperial eagle has also been recorded as nesting on electric poles and towers in Russia and Kazakhstan. In several cases they aggressively took over the nests from <a href="/wiki/Steppe_eagle" title="Steppe eagle">steppe eagles</a> that were previously nesting on the electric towers.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> Often one nest is used but sometimes a 2nd or 3rd is constructed by the imperial eagle pair may exist on a home range.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Fefelov_60-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fefelov-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aquila_heliaca_MWNH_0830.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Aquila_heliaca_MWNH_0830.JPG/220px-Aquila_heliaca_MWNH_0830.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="158" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Aquila_heliaca_MWNH_0830.JPG/330px-Aquila_heliaca_MWNH_0830.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Aquila_heliaca_MWNH_0830.JPG/440px-Aquila_heliaca_MWNH_0830.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3410" data-file-height="2453" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aquila_heliaca_MWNH_0830.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Egg, Collection <a href="/wiki/Museum_Wiesbaden" title="Museum Wiesbaden">Museum Wiesbaden</a></div></div></div>
<p>The mother eastern imperial eagle lays 2 to 3 eggs at intervals of 2 or more days. The average clutch size in <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a> was 2.09.<sup id="cite_ref-Abuladze_75-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abuladze-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a>, the average clutch size was 2.27.<sup id="cite_ref-Horal_69-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horal-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> 81 nests from Russia had a mean clutch size of 2.1.<sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin_36-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Exceptional clutch sizes of up to four eggs have been recorded in Kazakhstan.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> The eggs are a dull buffish white colour overall and are sparsely marked with grey, purplish or, occasionally, brown spots. A sample of 150 eggs were found to have measured from 63 to 82.5&#160;mm (2.48 to 3.25&#160;in) in height by 52.5 to 62.5&#160;mm (2.07 to 2.46&#160;in), with an average of 73.3&#160;mm ×&#160;56.5&#160;mm (2.89&#160;in ×&#160;2.22&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> In Georgia, the average dimensions of eggs was measured in a sample of 20 as 72.3&#160;mm ×&#160;57.5&#160;mm (2.85&#160;in ×&#160;2.26&#160;in) with a mean weight of 136.8&#160;g (4.83&#160;oz).<sup id="cite_ref-Abuladze_75-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abuladze-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> As was recorded in 13 cases in <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">Slovakia</a>, if eggs are lost early into incubation, around March or April, a replacement clutch may be laid, though always the parent eagles used an alternate nest when this occurred.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Europe" title="Southeast Europe">southeast Europe</a> egg laying peaks at about mid-February to March while in <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">central Asia</a>, it is in first half of April shifting late April further north.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Abuladze_75-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abuladze-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> The incubation starts with the 1st egg, with males only infrequently taking a shift. The incubation stage lasts for 43 days.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a>, two females were recorded to do 90.8-94.1% of the incubating.<sup id="cite_ref-Dobrev_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dobrev-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> The eaglets hatch at intervals of several days, with one usually being distinctly larger than the rest. However, cases of <a href="/wiki/Siblicide" title="Siblicide">siblicide</a> are infrequent compared to the golden eagle and, when not interrupted by human interference, prey population crashes and nest collapses, nest frequently bear two fledglings.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> At 14 days of age, the first feathers through down, while feathers on the back starting at 21 days. Feathers cover the down by 35–40 days but sometimes the down persists about head and neck for up to 45 days. Feathering is complete at 55 days and first flight attempts by 60 days onward. Mostly the female broods the young and male captures prey in this species (as is often the case in <a href="/wiki/Aquilinae" title="Aquilinae">booted eagles</a> in which males rarely engage in direct brooding).<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> From when the young are 40 days onward, the female eastern imperial eagle typically resumes hunting and takes to a perch nearby. Male does not appear to bring prey directly to the nest instead to nearby branch for female to dismantle.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dobrev_129-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dobrev-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> One or two large prey items last 1–2 days but small prey may require up to 5-6 daily prey deliveries.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dobrev_129-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dobrev-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> Fledgling may variously occur at anywhere from 63 to 77 days.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> After leaving the nest the young linger near it for 2–3 days. Breeding success was once considerable in Russia at about 1.5 young per pair.<sup id="cite_ref-Brown_16-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Breeding_success_and_survivorship">Breeding success and survivorship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_imperial_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Breeding success and survivorship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Imperial_Eagle_01.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Imperial_Eagle_01.JPG/220px-Imperial_Eagle_01.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Imperial_Eagle_01.JPG/330px-Imperial_Eagle_01.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Imperial_Eagle_01.JPG/440px-Imperial_Eagle_01.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1587" data-file-height="2112" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Imperial_Eagle_01.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A captive adult eastern imperial eagle.</div></div></div>
<p>Breeding success is somewhat variable in the eastern imperial eagle. In the Czech Republic the 1-3 pairs found to be nesting in 1998-2009 had an average number of 1.53 fledglings.<sup id="cite_ref-Horal_69-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horal-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> In the Hungarian population, it was recorded that from fewer than one fledgling per pair that average had increased to 1.15 fledglings per pair in 2001-2009.<sup id="cite_ref-Horvath3_62-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horvath3-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> In 2011 in <a href="/wiki/Bulgaria" title="Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a>, with a gradual increase of the population to 20 occupied territories, success rate was shown to be about 1 fledgling per pair. By 2014, productivity was reported as averaging similarly at 1.03 fledgling per pair in Bulgaria.<sup id="cite_ref-Bulgaria_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bulgaria-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Increase_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Increase-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> In Austria, the average number of fledglings per successful pair was 1.63. Of 27 breeding Austrian breeding attempts, 22 were successful.<sup id="cite_ref-Wichmann_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wichmann-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/East_Thrace" title="East Thrace">East Thrace</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>, the breeding success was estimated at 1.01 fledglings per pair. Pairs living in the <a href="/wiki/Marmara_region" class="mw-redirect" title="Marmara region">Marmara region</a> were more productive than those in the Dervent heights (1.05 vs 0.91).<sup id="cite_ref-Demerdzhiev_74-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Demerdzhiev-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Breeding success in Hungary reportedly was driven largely by the  age of the nesting pair, with mature adults more likely to show adaptability to changes in habitat and prey, and secondarily to habitat composition. In particular, nesting success tends to higher in lowland areas against higher elevation nests.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>  Post-dispersal in Bulgaria, the most significant cause of juvenile mortality per radio-tagging studies was shown to be electrocution (59% of the mortalities), followed by poisoning and shooting. The radio-tracking studies of Bulgarian juveniles showed survival rates were 59.1% in the 1st year of life, 83.3% for the 2nd year, 80% for the 3rd year. Furthermore, 50% of the mortalities of the Bulgarian juveniles occurred in Bulgaria while 43% occurred after they’d migrated to Turkey.<sup id="cite_ref-Bulgaria_133-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bulgaria-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Stoychev_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stoychev-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> Electrocution by collision with power lines are likely major causes of mortality almost throughout the range. Furthermore, other deadly collisions with manmade objects, including <a href="/wiki/Traffic_collision" title="Traffic collision">automobiles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wind_turbine" title="Wind turbine">wind turbines</a>, are also a persistent threat.<sup id="cite_ref-Haraszthy_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haraszthy-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> Like other birds of prey, eastern imperial eagles are occasionally vulnerable to <a href="/wiki/Haematozoa" title="Haematozoa">Haematozoa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Helminth" class="mw-redirect" title="Helminth">helminths</a> and viruses as well as maggot infection in a wound by <a href="/wiki/Calliphora_vicina" title="Calliphora vicina">blowflies</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> Studies to determine if there’s a skewered sex ratio of offspring for eastern imperial eagles in Kazakhstan and secondarily in Serbia determined that the ratio of males to females was nearly equal and stable. For example pairs in the <a href="/wiki/Naurzum_Nature_Reserve" title="Naurzum Nature Reserve">Naurzum Nature Reserve</a> showed during study to produce 123 males and 116 females from 1998-2004.<sup id="cite_ref-Dimorphism_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dimorphism-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> Further study in Kazakhstan showed that adult survivorship was about 84%, which is somewhat low for long-lived raptor, but nonetheless reproductive rate shown to be sufficient to maintain a stable breeding population. While breeding success in areas such as Naurzum reserve may be successful, in non-pristine habitats of Kazakhstan the breeding population may not necessarily be self-sustaining.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Katzner4_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Katzner4-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> In Bulgaria, estimates of adult survivorship were from 75 to 94%.<sup id="cite_ref-Bulgaria_133-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bulgaria-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Increase_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Increase-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Stoychev_137-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stoychev-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> Against the overall stability of Kazakhstani imperial eagles and the gradual increased success of European nesting eagles, a rapidly declining population has been noted with alarm, in nearly at the species’ eastern limits as breeder in <a href="/wiki/Lake_Baikal" title="Lake Baikal">Lake Baikal</a>. Here fledgling success has decreased from roughly 71% to about 52%.<sup id="cite_ref-Fefelov_60-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fefelov-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Baikal_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baikal-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Status">Status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Eastern_imperial_eagle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Eastern_Imperial_Eagle_(31083627027).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Eastern_Imperial_Eagle_%2831083627027%29.jpg/220px-Eastern_Imperial_Eagle_%2831083627027%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="286" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Eastern_Imperial_Eagle_%2831083627027%29.jpg/330px-Eastern_Imperial_Eagle_%2831083627027%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Eastern_Imperial_Eagle_%2831083627027%29.jpg/440px-Eastern_Imperial_Eagle_%2831083627027%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="862" data-file-height="1119" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Eastern_Imperial_Eagle_(31083627027).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A juvenile eastern imperial eagle in <a href="/wiki/Rajasthan" title="Rajasthan">Rajasthan</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a></div></div></div>
<p>The eastern imperial eagle has declined greatly through history. At one time, people of <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">eastern Europe</a> and adjacent parts of Russia used to admire and even revere this eagle as a "sacred bird". It was widely referred to as the "cross-bearing eagle" (because of the shape of the white spots on its wings).<sup id="cite_ref-Petrov_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Petrov-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> According to folklore, the imperial eagle was able to divert the hailstorm clouds from the area it inhabited, and it was thus able to save farmers' crops. Therefore, the monarchy of <a href="/wiki/Austria-Hungary" title="Austria-Hungary">Austria-Hungary</a> once chose the imperial eagle to be its <a href="/wiki/Heraldry" title="Heraldry">heraldic</a> animal.<sup id="cite_ref-Petrov_148-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Petrov-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> Despite the historic reverence for the species, it was not spared the negative and hostile view of all birds of prey that arose throughout Europe and its colonies in the 17th century onwards, and the resulting persecution of essentially all birds of prey, including the eastern imperial eagle.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> However, almost certainly the leading cause of this species' decline was the practice of felling old trees on field boundaries that were the nest sites for the eagle. Among other reasons for its decline were the depletion (both incidental and intentional) of prey species and the incidental consumption by the eagles of poison baits, left out to eliminate <a href="/wiki/Gray_wolf" class="mw-redirect" title="Gray wolf">wolves</a>. As with other eagles in Eurasia, the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_revolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Industrial revolution">Industrial revolution</a> allowed access to <a href="/wiki/Poison" title="Poison">poisons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Firearm" title="Firearm">firearms</a>, which made the killing of eagles far easier and hastened their decline.<sup id="cite_ref-Petrov_148-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Petrov-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Belik_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Belik-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> Towards the end of the 19th century, 1824 nests were recorded in Bulgaria, but by 1979 only 5 to 12 pairs remained in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-Petrov_148-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Petrov-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> Similarly, from a population once numbering in the thousands, the Hungarian population of imperial eagles was driven down to only 10-15 pairs by 1975-1980.<sup id="cite_ref-Haraszthy_70-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haraszthy-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a>, the eastern imperial eagle was extirpated altogether as a breeding species.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> While the declines further east in the species range have been less studied, more or less the same threats likely persist throughout the range. Some reserves in central Russia and Kazakhstan have retained semi-stable populations, due in no small part to their isolation.<sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin_36-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> However, the eastern imperial eagle has also been extirpated as a breeding species from <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ferguson-Lees_2-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ferguson-Lees-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Khan_122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Khan-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Today, dedicated conservationists and biologists are working extensively to remediate and reverse the decline of eastern imperial eagle. The species was uplisted to <a href="/wiki/Vulnerable_species" title="Vulnerable species">Vulnerable</a> in 1994 and global protection initiatives were begun around this time.<sup id="cite_ref-iucn_1-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iucn-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> By 1996, it was estimated that there were 363-604 pairs in all of Europe (including <a href="/wiki/European_Russia" title="European Russia">European Russia</a>). After a couple of decades of recovery efforts, by 2000-2010, the number of breeding pairs in Europe was estimated at up to 1800-2200, a significant increase. Peak numbers in Europe may fall along the <a href="/wiki/European_Green_Belt" title="European Green Belt">European Green Belt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> The strongest increases were in Hungary where from 10-15 pairs in the late 1970s an estimated total of 105 nesting pairs by the late 2000s. Conversions and conservation of lowland wildlands, has allowed the numbers of pairs using less ideal mountainous habitat decrease from 50% to 15% of breeding imperials, as they’ve shifted to more ideal lowland habitat. The nation’s imperial eagle population has been continuously protected and monitored since 1975. Further efforts in Hungary were the mitigation of human-eagle conflicts through education in Hungary as poisoning (whether these eagles are the main target or not) continues, as well as general coordination with landowners and agricultural interests as the eastern imperial eagles are often required to forage in such areas. Other increases or returns to former parts of their range include have been noted in the <a href="/wiki/Morava_(river)" title="Morava (river)">Morava region</a> of southwestern <a href="/wiki/Slovakia" title="Slovakia">Slovakia</a> as well as some parts of the <a href="/wiki/Czech_Republic" title="Czech Republic">Czech Republic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Horal_69-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Horal-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wichmann_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wichmann-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> However, overall 85% of the population in the <a href="/wiki/Carpathian_mountains" class="mw-redirect" title="Carpathian mountains">Carpathian mountains</a> region that forms the bulk of the land in eastern Europe was restricted to the <a href="/wiki/Great_Hungarian_Plain" title="Great Hungarian Plain">Great Hungarian Plain</a>, and were isolated from other populations. In Slovakia, the gap in the distribution in the <a href="/wiki/Carpathian_basin" class="mw-redirect" title="Carpathian basin">Carpathian basin</a> has led to concern about genetic isolation. Upon review, the genetic isolation of the two Slovakian populations was determined to be "marginally significant".<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> The number of pairs of eastern imperial eagles increased in Bulgaria from 20 in 2009 to 24 in 2013. Among the efforts untaken to restore the Bulgarian populations were mitigating hazardous electrical poles, 608 were insulated within eagle territories, while also 483 supplemental feedings were given to 14 pairs from October to March.<sup id="cite_ref-Bulgaria_133-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bulgaria-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Increase_134-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Increase-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Stoychev_137-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stoychev-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> In Turkey, the number of imperial eagle pairs in recent estimates during the 21st century was widely placed at 35-150 pairs, probably closer to the higher number as there is evidence for there being 30-50 pairs in the <a href="/wiki/East_Thrace" title="East Thrace">East Thrace</a> region alone.<sup id="cite_ref-Demerdzhiev_74-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Demerdzhiev-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Demerdzhiev2_121-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Demerdzhiev2-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Turan,_L._2005_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Turan,_L._2005-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Placing_artificial_nests_for_Eastern_Imperial_Eagle.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Placing_artificial_nests_for_Eastern_Imperial_Eagle.jpg/220px-Placing_artificial_nests_for_Eastern_Imperial_Eagle.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="331" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Placing_artificial_nests_for_Eastern_Imperial_Eagle.jpg/330px-Placing_artificial_nests_for_Eastern_Imperial_Eagle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Placing_artificial_nests_for_Eastern_Imperial_Eagle.jpg/440px-Placing_artificial_nests_for_Eastern_Imperial_Eagle.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="3008" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Placing_artificial_nests_for_Eastern_Imperial_Eagle.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Biologists working to place an artificial nests for imperial eagles to use.</div></div></div>
<p>In some areas of western Russia and in Kazakhstan, the eastern imperial eagle has maintained the nearest thing to a population stronghold. In 2011, the total estimated number of pairs was estimated at 3000-3500 in Russia and 3500-4000 in Kazakhstan. Though only 1534 breeding territories found firsthand, only some parts of the range were surveyed. According to Russian and Kazakh studies, cattle grazing in <a href="/wiki/Taiga" title="Taiga">taiga</a> forest edge appeared to benefit imperial eagles since they encourage habitat for prey, especially <a href="/wiki/Ground_squirrel" title="Ground squirrel">ground squirrels</a>. In Kazakhstan moister conditions, possibly correlated with <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" class="mw-redirect" title="Global warming">climate change</a> seems to have increased populations of <a href="/wiki/Yellow_ground_squirrel" title="Yellow ground squirrel">yellow ground squirrels</a> and 2nd growth trees which both are likely to have caused a localized increase there.<sup id="cite_ref-Michev_34-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Michev-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Karyakin_36-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karyakin-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Katzner2_80-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Katzner2-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Naurzum_Nature_Reserve" title="Naurzum Nature Reserve">Naurzum Nature Reserve</a> alone nearly 300 imperial eagles may congregate in winter, making it a “critical refugium” for this species.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> On the other hand, precipitous decline has been detected in the <a href="/wiki/Lake_Baikal" title="Lake Baikal">Lake Baikal</a> region of Russia and the species may be on its way to local extinction there. In the 1950s, imperial eagle pairs in the Baikal area numbered 250-300 strong. A strong decline was already noted by the mid-1980s when 150-200 pairs remained. However, the reduction hastened even more to merely 70 pairs in 1999. The Baikal imperial eagles during monitoring from 1950 to 1999 showed no overall changes in habitat or pair occupancy. In the 1998-1999 period, fledgling success was also found to drop considerably. Satellite study of migrating birds from Lake Baikal at migration stopovers and winter sites was reviewed but only 4 eagles were studied so results could not clearly determine whether depletions were mostly occurring at this time rather than during breeding. The reason for the sharp declines of the Baikal imperial eagles are not fully understood but are likely correlated with habitat destruction and human land use changes followed by pesticide use and other toxic threats through bioaccumulation.<sup id="cite_ref-Fefelov_60-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fefelov-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Baikal_147-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Baikal-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> Reductions have also been reported in the lower <a href="/wiki/Ural_mountains" class="mw-redirect" title="Ural mountains">Ural mountains</a> region of Russia with largely similar threats but it is not known what the exact rate of reduction is here.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> Although gone from <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> as a breeding species, it continues to be a key wintering site as around 150 eagles are estimated to winter in Pakistan.<sup id="cite_ref-Khan_122-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Khan-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-Forsman-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Forsman_3-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Forsman_3-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Forsman_3-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Forsman_3-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Forsman_3-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Forsman_3-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Forsman_3-6"><sup><i><b>g</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Forsman, D. <li id="cite_note-HBW-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-HBW_4-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-HBW_4-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-HBW_4-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-HBW_4-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-HBW_4-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-HBW_4-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-HBW_4-6"><sup><i><b>g</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-HBW_4-7"><sup><i><b>h</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-HBW_4-8"><sup><i><b>i</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite id="CITEREFMeyburg1994" class="citation book cs1">Meyburg, B.U.
